<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The barley pan-genome reveals the hidden legacy of mutation breeding"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature 2020 588:7837"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-11-25"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Genetic diversity is key to crop improvement. Owing to pervasive genomic structural variation, a single reference genome assembly cannot capture the full complement of sequence diversity of a crop species (known as the &#8216;pan-genome&#8217;1). Multiple high-quality sequence assemblies are an indispensable component of a pan-genome infrastructure. Barley (Hordeum vulgare L.) is an important cereal crop with a long history of cultivation that is adapted to a wide range of agro-climatic conditions2. Here we report the construction of chromosome-scale sequence assemblies for the genotypes of 20&#160;varieties of barley&#8212;comprising landraces, cultivars and a wild barley&#8212;that were selected as representatives of global barley diversity. We catalogued genomic presence/absence variants and explored the use of structural variants for quantitative genetic analysis through whole-genome shotgun sequencing of 300&#160;gene bank accessions. We discovered abundant large inversion polymorphisms and analysed in detail two inversions that are frequently found in current elite barley germplasm; one is probably the product of mutation breeding and the other is tightly linked to a locus that is involved in the expansion of geographical range. This first-generation barley pan-genome makes previously hidden genetic variation accessible to genetic studies and breeding. Chromosome-scale sequence assemblies of 20&#160;diverse varieties of barley are used to construct a first-generation pan-genome, revealing previously hidden genetic variation that can be used by studies aimed at crop improvement"/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Genetic diversity is key to crop improvement. Owing to pervasive genomic structural variation, a single reference genome assembly cannot capture the full complement of sequence diversity of a crop species (known as the âpan-genomeâ<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Bayer, P. E., Golicz, A. A., Scheben, A., Batley, J. &amp; Edwards, D. Plant pan-genomes are the new reference. Nat. Plants 6, 914â920 (2020)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1185">1</a></sup>). Multiple high-quality sequence assemblies are an indispensable component of a pan-genome infrastructure. Barley (<i>Hordeum vulgare</i> L.) is an important cereal crop with a long history of cultivation that is adapted to a wide range of agro-climatic conditions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Dawson, I. K. et al. Barley: a translational model for adaptation to climate change. New Phytol. 206, 913â931 (2015)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1192">2</a></sup>. Here we report the construction of chromosome-scale sequence assemblies for the genotypes of 20Â varieties of barleyâcomprising landraces, cultivars and a wild barleyâthat were selected as representatives of global barley diversity. We catalogued genomic presence/absence variants and explored the use of structural variants for quantitative genetic analysis through whole-genome shotgun sequencing of 300Â gene bank accessions. We discovered abundant large inversion polymorphisms and analysed in detail two inversions that are frequently found in current elite barley germplasm; one is probably the product of mutation breeding and the other is tightly linked to a locus that is involved in the expansion of geographical range. This first-generation barley pan-genome makes previously hidden genetic variation accessible to genetic studies and breeding.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>A staple food of ancient civilizations, today barley is used mainly for animal feed and malting. Barley is more adaptable to harsh environmental conditions than its close relative wheat, and maintains an important role in human nutrition in harsh climatic regions that include the Ethiopian and Tibetan highlands<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Dawson, I. K. et al. Barley: a translational model for adaptation to climate change. New Phytol. 206, 913â931 (2015)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1218">2</a></sup>. As in other crops, genomics has been a major driver of progress in barley genetics and breeding in the past decade<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Stein, N. &amp; Muehlbauer, G. J. The Barley Genome (Springer, 2018)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1222">3</a></sup>. The first draft reference genome for barley<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="International Barley Genome Sequencing Consortium. A physical, genetic and functional sequence assembly of the barley genome. Nature 491, 711â716 (2012)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1226">4</a></sup>, and its subsequent revisions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Mascher, M. et al. A chromosome conformation capture ordered sequence of the barley genome. Nature 544, 427â433 (2017)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1230">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Monat, C. et al. TRITEX: chromosome-scale sequence assembly of Triticeae genomes with open-source tools. Genome Biol. 20, 284 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1233">6</a></sup>, have formed the basis for gene isolation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Mascher, M. et al. Mapping-by-sequencing accelerates forward genetics in barley. Genome Biol. 15, R78 (2014)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1237">7</a></sup>, compiling a single-nucleotide polymorphism (SNP) variation atlas for wild and domesticated germplasm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Russell, J. et al. Exome sequencing of geographically diverse barley landraces and wild relatives gives insights into environmental adaptation. Nat. Genet. 48, 1024â1030 (2016)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1242">8</a></sup>, and activating plant genetic resources<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Milner, S. G. et al. Genebank genomics highlights the diversity of a global barley collection. Nat. Genet. 51, 319â326 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1246">9</a></sup>. At the same time, reduced-representation surveys of structural variation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="MuÃ±oz-AmatriaÃ­n, M. et al. Distribution, functional impact, and origin mechanisms of copy number variation in the barley genome. Genome Biol. 14, R58 (2013)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1250">10</a></sup> and map-based cloning<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Taketa, S. et al. Barley grain with adhering hulls is controlled by an ERF family transcription factor gene regulating a lipid biosynthesis pathway. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 105, 4062â4067 (2008)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1254">11</a></sup> have implicated variation in gene content and copy number in the control of agronomic traits. The concept of the pan-genome refers to a species-wide catalogue of genic presence/absence variation (PAV)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Tettelin, H. et al. Genome analysis of multiple pathogenic isolates of Streptococcus agalactiae: implications for the microbial âpan-genomeâ. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 102, 13950â13955 (2005)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1258">12</a></sup>, or more generally, structural variation that affects (potentially non-coding) sequences of 50Â or more base pairs (bp) in size. Although several methods of pan-genomic analysis that use short-read sequence data in the context of a single reference genome have been devised<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Ho, S. S., Urban, A. E. &amp; Mills, R. E. Structural variation in the sequencing era. Nat. Rev. Genet. 21, 171â189 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1262">13</a></sup>, large and complex genomes require multiple high-quality sequence assemblies to capture and contextualize sequences that are absent inâor highly diverged fromâa single reference genotype<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Danilevicz, M. F., Tay Fernandez, C. G., Marsh, J. I., Bayer, P. E. &amp; Edwards, D. Plant pangenomics: approaches, applications and advancements. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 54, 18â25 (2020)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1267">14</a></sup>. Progress in sequencing and genome mapping technologies has only recently made possible the fast and cost-effective assembly of tens of genotypes of large-genome plant species, such as barley (haploid genome size of 5Â Gb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Monat, C., Schreiber, M., Stein, N. &amp; Mascher, M. Prospects of pan-genomics in barley. Theor. Appl. Genet. 132, 785â796 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1271">15</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Twenty barley reference genomes"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Twenty barley reference genomes</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><p>The starting point for pan-genomics in barley was the comprehensive survey of species-wide diversity on the basis of the genome-wide genotyping of more than 22,000Â barley accessions, mainly from the German national gene bank<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Milner, S. G. et al. Genebank genomics highlights the diversity of a global barley collection. Nat. Genet. 51, 319â326 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1283">9</a></sup>. To achieve a good representation of major barley gene pools, we selected accessions that were located in the branches of the first six principal components from the previously published principal component analysis (PCA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Milner, S. G. et al. Genebank genomics highlights the diversity of a global barley collection. Nat. Genet. 51, 319â326 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1287">9</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Fig1">1a</a>, Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Fig5">1</a>), reflecting the key determinants of population structure: geographical origin, row type and annual growth habit. In addition to these gene pool representatives, our panel included the reference cultivar Morex<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Mascher, M. et al. A chromosome conformation capture ordered sequence of the barley genome. Nature 544, 427â433 (2017)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1297">5</a></sup>, two current or former elite malting varieties (RGT Planet and Hockett), two founder lines of Chinese barley breeding (ZDM01467 and ZDM02064), Golden Promise and Igri (two genotypes with high transformation efficiency<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Coronado, M.-J., Hensel, G., Broeders, S., Otto, I. &amp; Kumlehn, J. Immature pollen-derived doubled haploid formation in barley cv. Golden Promise as a tool for transgene recombination. Acta Physiol. Plant. 27, 591â599 (2005)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1302">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Schreiber, M. et al. A genome assembly of the barley âtransformation referenceâ cultivar Golden Promise. G3 10, 1823â1827 (2020)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1305">17</a></sup>), Barke (a successful German variety and the parent of several mutant and mapping populations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Gottwald, S., Bauer, P., Komatsuda, T., Lundqvist, U. &amp; Stein, N. TILLING in the two-rowed barley cultivar âBarkeâ reveals preferred sites of functional diversity in the gene HvHox1. BMC Res. Notes 2, 258 (2009)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1309">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Mascher, M. et al. Anchoring and ordering NGS contig assemblies by population sequencing (POPSEQ). Plant J. 76, 718â727 (2013)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1312">19</a></sup>) and one wild barley (<i>H. vulgare</i> subsp. <i>spontaneum</i> (K. Koch) Thell.) genotype from Israel (B1K-04-12, a desert ecotype collected at Ein Prat)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="HÃ¼bner, S. et al. Strong correlation of wild barley (Hordeum spontaneum) population structure with temperature and precipitation variation. Mol. Ecol. 18, 1523â1536 (2009)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1322">20</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Chromosome-scale sequences of 20Â representative barley genotypes reveal large structural variants."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Chromosome-scale sequences of 20Â representative barley genotypes reveal large structural variants.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-020-2947-8/MediaObjects/41586_2020_2947_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-020-2947-8/MediaObjects/41586_2020_2947_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="276" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>, We selected 20Â barley genotypes to represent the genetic diversity space, as revealed by PCA of genotyping-by-sequencing data of 19,778Â domesticated varieties of barley<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Milner, S. G. et al. Genebank genomics highlights the diversity of a global barley collection. Nat. Genet. 51, 319â326 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1340">9</a></sup>. Principal component (PC)3 and PC4 are shown. The proportion of variance explained by the principal components is indicated in the axis labels. Further principal components are shown in Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Fig5">1a</a>. <b>b</b>, Alignment of the pseudomolecules of chromosome 2H of the Morex and Barke cultivars. The inset zooms in on a 10-Mb inversion that is frequently found in germplasm from northern Europe. Co-linearity plots for all assemblies and chromosomes are shown in Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Fig7">3a</a>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We constructed chromosome-scale sequence assemblies for 20Â accessions (Extended Data Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Tab1">1</a>). In brief, paired-end and mate-pair Illumina short reads were assembled into scaffolds of megabase (Mb)-scale contiguity (Extended Data Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Tab1">1</a>). Scaffold assembly was done with Minia<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Chikhi, R., Limasset, A. &amp; Medvedev, P. Compacting de Bruijn graphs from sequencing data quickly and in low memory. Bioinformatics 32, i201âi208 (2016)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1370">21</a></sup> and SOAPDenovo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1374">22</a></sup> following the TRITEX method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Monat, C. et al. TRITEX: chromosome-scale sequence assembly of Triticeae genomes with open-source tools. Genome Biol. 20, 284 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1378">6</a></sup> (<i>n</i>Â =Â 16), DeNovoMagic from NRGene (<i>n</i>Â =Â 3) or W2rap<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Clavijo, B. J. et al. An improved assembly and annotation of the allohexaploid wheat genome identifies complete families of agronomic genes and provides genomic evidence for chromosomal translocations. Genome Res. 27, 885â896 (2017)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1389">23</a></sup> (<i>n</i>Â =Â 1). We used 10X Genomics Chromium linked-reads and chromosome conformation capture (Hi-C) data to arrange scaffolds into chromosomal pseudomolecules using the TRITEX pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Monat, C. et al. TRITEX: chromosome-scale sequence assembly of Triticeae genomes with open-source tools. Genome Biol. 20, 284 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1396">6</a></sup> (Extended Data Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Tab1">1</a>). A comparison of the short-read assembly of the Morex cultivar to a long-read assembly of this genotype generated from PacBio long reads showed high co-linearity at chromosomal scale, good concordance in gene space representation and similar power to detect PAV (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Fig6">2</a>), indicating that short-read assemblies are amenable to pan-genomic analyses in barley. Although the assemblies of the 20Â diverse accessions differed in contiguity and the extent of gap sequence in the intergenic space, they had a similar representation of reference gene models (Morex V2) and were highly co-linear to each other at the whole-chromosome scale (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Fig1">1b</a>, Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Fig7">3</a>). A similar proportion (about 80%) of the assembled sequence of each genotype was composed of transposable elements, with an average of 113,200Â intact full-length long-terminal repeat retro-elements (LTRs) per assembly (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#MOESM6">1</a>). However, we found pronounced differences in the number of shared intact full-length LTR locations: only 17 to 25% of full-length LTR locations present in the wild barley B1K-04-12 were shared at 98% sequence identity and 98% alignment coverage with any domesticated genotype (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Fig8">4</a>). By contrast, more closely related domesticated genotypes shared between 53% and 67% of their full-length LTRs, consistent with previous reports of rapid sequence turn-over in the non-coding space in large-genome plant species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Anderson, S. N. et al. Transposable elements contribute to dynamic genome content in maize. Plant J. 100, 1052â1065 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1419">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Brunner, S., Fengler, K., Morgante, M., Tingey, S. &amp; Rafalski, A. Evolution of DNA sequence nonhomologies among maize inbreds. Plant Cell 17, 343â360 (2005)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1422">25</a></sup>.</p><p>De novo gene annotation using Illumina RNA sequencing and PacBio Iso-Seq data (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#MOESM7">2</a>) was performed for three genotypes: Morex (which has previously been reported<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Monat, C. et al. TRITEX: chromosome-scale sequence assembly of Triticeae genomes with open-source tools. Genome Biol. 20, 284 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1432">6</a></sup>), Barke and the Ethiopian landrace HORÂ 10350 (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Fig9">5</a>). Gene models defined on the basis of these three assemblies were consolidated and projected onto the remaining 17Â assemblies (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Fig9">5</a>). Between 35,859 and 40,044Â gene models were annotated by projection in each assembly (Extended Data Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Tab1">1</a>) with an average of 37,515 (s.d.Â =Â 896). The number of gene models was about 20% higher in the projections than in de novo annotations (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Fig9">5e</a>), which indicates that some of the models lack transcript support: possible explanations for the discrepancy are highly tissue-specific expression or pseudogenization. The clustering of orthologous gene models yielded 40,176Â orthologous groups. Of these, 21,992Â occurred as a single copy in all 20Â assemblies; 3,236Â occurred in multiple copies in at least one of the 20Â assemblies; 13,188Â were absent from at least one assembly; and 1,760Â were present in only one assembly. On average, 14.7% of gene models annotated in each assembly occurred in tandem arrays that comprised two or more adjacent copies. These results point to abundant genic copy-number variation between barley genotypes. Future transcriptomic studies will ascertain the effect of structural variants on gene expression.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec3" data-title="Pan-genome as a tool for genetics and breeding"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec3-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec3">Pan-genome as a tool for genetics and breeding</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec3-content"><p>High-quality genome assemblies are a resource for ascertaining and providing context to structural variants, which can then be genotyped in a wider set of germplasm using low-coverage or reduced-representation sequence data. We used two complementary approaches to detect structural variation: assembly comparison and clustering of single-copy sequences to derive markers that can be scored in short-read data. We used the Assemblytics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Nattestad, M. &amp; Schatz, M. C. Assemblytics: a web analytics tool for the detection of variants from an assembly. Bioinformatics 32, 3021â3023 (2016)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1457">26</a></sup> software to discover PAV by pair-wise comparison of 19Â chromosome-scale assemblies to the Morex reference. We identified 1,586,262Â PAVs, ranging in size from 50 to 999,568Â bp, and observed an enrichment for low-frequency variants (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Fig10">6a, b</a>). PAV density was higher in distal, gene-rich regions (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Fig10">6c</a>), which are characterized by higher nucleotide diversity and recombination rates<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Russell, J. et al. Exome sequencing of geographically diverse barley landraces and wild relatives gives insights into environmental adaptation. Nat. Genet. 48, 1024â1030 (2016)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1467">8</a></sup>. A total of 5,446 out of 5,602Â deletions longer than 5Â kilobases (kb) found in Barke relative to Morex were mapped genetically in the 90Â recombinant inbred lines of the MorexÂ ÃÂ Barke population<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Mascher, M. et al. Anchoring and ordering NGS contig assemblies by population sequencing (POPSEQ). Plant J. 76, 718â727 (2013)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1471">19</a></sup> with highly concordant positions (Spearman correlationÂ =Â 0.99) (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Fig10">6d</a>), which provides support for the accuracy of the detected polymorphisms. At least one member of 18,562 (46%) groups of orthologous genes overlapped with structural variants discovered in the 20Â sequence assemblies. As observed in other plant species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Gordon, S. P. et al. Extensive gene content variation in the Brachypodium distachyon pan-genome correlates with population structure. Nat. Commun. 8, 2184 (2017)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1479">27</a></sup>, resistance-gene homologues containing NB-ARC and protein kinase domains were frequently found among PAV genes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#MOESM8">3</a>).</p><p>Structural variants cover non-genic regions composed of repetitive sequence, making it hard to establish orthologous relationships or the presence of specific alleles from short-read data only. To derive quantitative estimates of the extent of pan-genomic variation and as a tool for genetic analysis such as association scans, we focused on single-copy regions extracted from each of the 20Â assemblies and clustered into a non-redundant set of sequences (hereafter referred to as the âsingle-copy pan-genomeâ) (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Fig11">7a</a>). The average cumulative size of single-copy sequence in each accession was 478Â Mb (that is, 9.5% of the assembly genome). The total size of non-redundant single-copy sequence was 638.6Â Mb, represented by 1,472,508Â clusters with an N50 of 1,087Â bp (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Fig11">7b</a>). The single-copy sequence shared among all 20Â genotypes amounted to 402.5Â Mb, whereas 235.9Â Mb were variable (that is, absent or present in higher copy number in at least one assembly) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Fig2">2a</a>). On average, each of the 20Â genotypes contained 2.9Â Mb of single-copy sequence not present in any other assembly. As observed for transposable element divergence, the wild barley B1K-04-12 had the highest amount of unique single-copy sequence (Extended Data Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Tab1">1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Single-copy pan-genome and use of PAVs in association mapping."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Single-copy pan-genome and use of PAVs in association mapping.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-020-2947-8/MediaObjects/41586_2020_2947_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-020-2947-8/MediaObjects/41586_2020_2947_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="714" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Cumulative size of single-copy regions in genome assemblies of 20Â barley genotypes. The genotypes were ordered according to the size of their unique single-copy sequence. <b>b</b>, Genome-wide association scan for lemma adherence on the basis of PAV markers. The black and red dots in the Manhattan plot denote single-copy sequences that are present and absent in Morex, respectively. <b>c</b>, The most highly associated PAV marker was a 16.7-kb region that is deleted in the naked accession HOR 7552 and that contains the <i>NUD</i> gene<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Taketa, S. et al. Barley grain with adhering hulls is controlled by an ERF family transcription factor gene regulating a lipid biosynthesis pathway. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 105, 4062â4067 (2008)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1524">11</a></sup>. <b>d</b>, Allelic status of the <i>NUD</i> deletion in 196Â domesticated varieties of barley. Normalized single-copy <i>k</i>-mer counts within the 16.7-kb region are shown for hulled (<i>n</i>Â =Â 160Â genotypes) and naked varieties (<i>n</i>Â =Â 36Â genotypes).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To test the suitability of the single-copy pan-genome for genetic analysis in a wider diversity panel without high-quality genome sequences, we collected whole-genome shotgun data (threefold coverage) for 200Â domesticated and 100Â wild varieties of barley (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#MOESM9">4</a>). The abundance of 160,716Â single-copy clusters that overlap structural variants was estimated by counting cluster-constituent <i>k</i>-mers (<i>k</i>Â =Â 31) in sequence reads of the diversity panel. In addition, we analysed genotyping-by-sequencing data of 19,778Â gene bank accessions of domesticated barley<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Milner, S. G. et al. Genebank genomics highlights the diversity of a global barley collection. Nat. Genet. 51, 319â326 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1564">9</a></sup> using the same approach. Abundance estimates based on <i>k</i>-mers (hereafter referred to as âpan-genome markersâ) showed that loci detected as single-copy sequence in one genome assembly can vary in copy number from zero to many in diverse germplasm (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Fig11">7c</a>). A PCA of pan-genome markers genotyped in whole-genome shotgun and genotyping-by-sequencing data highlighted the same drivers of global population structure as SNPs (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Fig11">7dâg</a>). In genome-wide association scans for morphological traits, pan-genome markers revealedâwith a good signal-to-noise ratioâpeaks that are consistent with previous reports<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Milner, S. G. et al. Genebank genomics highlights the diversity of a global barley collection. Nat. Genet. 51, 319â326 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1578">9</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Fig2">2b</a>, Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Fig12">8</a>). Notably, the pan-genome marker that was most highly associated with lemma adherence covered the <i>NUDUM</i> (<i>NUD</i>) gene<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Taketa, S. et al. Barley grain with adhering hulls is controlled by an ERF family transcription factor gene regulating a lipid biosynthesis pathway. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 105, 4062â4067 (2008)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1595">11</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Fig2">2c</a>). All varieties of naked barleyâin which lemmas can be easily separated from grainsâare thought to trace back to a single mutational event, deleting the entire <i>NUD</i> sequence<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Taketa, S. et al. Barley grain with adhering hulls is controlled by an ERF family transcription factor gene regulating a lipid biosynthesis pathway. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 105, 4062â4067 (2008)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1605">11</a></sup>. Another putative knockout allele of <i>NUD</i> (<i>nud1.g</i>) that contains a likely disruptive SNP variant was recently found in Tibetan barley<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Yu, S. et al. A single nucleotide polymorphism of Nud converts the caryopsis type of barley (Hordeum vulgare L.). Plant Mol. Biol. Report. 34, 242â248 (2016)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1616">28</a></sup>. All 36Â naked accessions in our panel contained the known deletion (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Fig2">2d</a>), indicating that broader sampling of barley diversityâwith a particular focus on centres of (morphological) diversityâis needed to discover novel rare alleles by genomic analyses.</p><p>Compared to reference-free approaches for <i>k</i>-mer-based genome-wide association scans such as AgRenSeq<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Arora, S. et al. Resistance gene cloning from a wild crop relative by sequence capture and association genetics. Nat. Biotechnol. 37, 139â143 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1629">29</a></sup>, trait-associated pan-genome markers are assigned with high precision to genomic positions, and aligning sequence assemblies in their vicinity provides immediate information about differences between haplotypes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Fig2">2c</a>). Furthermore, the reduction of marker number by implicit clustering of <i>k</i>-mers into single-copy loci allows the use of standard mixed linear models<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Lipka, A. E. et al. GAPIT: genome association and prediction integrated tool. Bioinformatics 28, 2397â2399 (2012)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1639">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Yu, J. et al. A unified mixed-model method for association mapping that accounts for multiple levels of relatedness. Nat. Genet. 38, 203â208 (2006)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1642">31</a></sup> to correct for genomic relatedness.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec4" data-title="A map of polymorphic inversions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec4-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec4">A map of polymorphic inversions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec4-content"><p>Chromosome-scale sequence assemblies can reveal large-scale rearrangements that are challenging to detect with other methods. Large inversions (more than 5Â Mb in size) were prominent in the genome alignments of our 20Â assemblies (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Fig1">1b</a>, Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Fig7">3a, c</a>). Previous reports on segregating inversions in barley are anecdotal and have focused on induced mutants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Ekberg, I. Cytogenetic studies of three paracentric inversions in barley. Hereditas 76, 1â30 (1974)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1660">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Ramage, R. &amp; Suneson, C. Translocation-gene linkages on barley chromosome 7. Crop Sci. 1, 319â320 (1961)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1663">33</a></sup>. To discover inversions in a broader set of germplasm, we mined patterns of contact frequencies in Hi-C data of a diversity panel mapped to a single reference genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Himmelbach, A. et al. Discovery of multi-megabase polymorphic inversions by chromosome conformation capture sequencing in large-genome plant species. Plant J. 96, 1309â1316 (2018)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1667">34</a></sup>. Among 69Â barley genotypes (67Â domesticated and 2Â wild accessions) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#MOESM10">5</a>), Hi-C-based inversion scans revealed a total of 42Â events that ranged from 4 to 141Â Mb in size (mean size of 23.9Â Mb) (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Fig13">9a</a>). Most of these events occurred in the low-recombining pericentromeric regions of the barley chromosomes and segregated at low frequency: 25Â events were observed only once (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Fig13">9b, c</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#MOESM11">6</a>). We focus here on two notable examples: a frequent event on chromosome 2H and an inversion in the distal region of the long arm of chromosome 7H.</p><p>The inversion in chromosomeÂ 7H detected in the RGT Planet cultivar was the largest event that segregated in our panel (141Â Mb) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Fig3">3a</a>). In a biparental mapping population derived from a cross between RGT Planet and the non-carrier cultivar Hindmarsh (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Fig3">3b</a>), this event repressed recombination in an interval that spanned 49Â cM in the genetic map of the MorexÂ ÃÂ Barke population<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Mascher, M. et al. Anchoring and ordering NGS contig assemblies by population sequencing (POPSEQ). Plant J. 76, 718â727 (2013)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1693">19</a></sup>, which is isogenic for absence of the inversion (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Fig3">3c</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#MOESM12">7</a>). We also observed a moderately distorted segregation (57% allele frequency, <i>Ï</i><sup>2</sup>Â =Â 4.88, <i>P</i>Â &lt;Â 0.05) in favour of the Hindmarsh allele in this interval. Recombination frequencies were increased in the flanking regions of the inversion in the RGT PlanetÂ ÃÂ Hindmarsh population relative to MorexÂ ÃÂ Barke, which suggests a compensatory mechanism to maintain an average number of one-to-two crossovers per chromosome in the presence of large tracts of suppressed recombination<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Ederveen, A., Lai, Y., van Driel, M. A., Gerats, T. &amp; Peters, J. L. Modulating crossover positioning by introducing large structural changes in chromosomes. BMC Genomics 16, 89 (2015)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1711">35</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Identification and characterization of a large inversion on chromosome 7H."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Identification and characterization of a large inversion on chromosome 7H.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-020-2947-8/MediaObjects/41586_2020_2947_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-020-2947-8/MediaObjects/41586_2020_2947_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="727" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Alignment of the 7H pseudomolecules of the Morex and RGT Planet cultivars. <b>b</b>, Alignment of physical and genetic positions mapped in the RGT PlanetÂ ÃÂ Hindmarsh (RÂ ÃÂ H) (left) and MorexÂ ÃÂ Barke (MÂ ÃÂ B) (right) populations. Red shading marks the inverted region. <b>c</b>, We converted genetic distances to recombination rates in the RÂ ÃÂ H (left) and MÂ ÃÂ B (right) populations. A single marker per recombination block is shown. <b>d</b>, We designed a PCR marker (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#MOESM1">1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#MOESM3">2a</a>) to screen for the presence of the inversion in gene bank accessions that represent the Valticky and Diamant cultivars.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>By focusing on the inversion breakpoints in the RGT Planet sequence assembly, we designed a diagnostic PCR assay (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#MOESM3">2a, b, d</a>) to rapidly genotype the presence of the inversion in 1,406Â accessions (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#MOESM13">8</a>). The inverted haplotype occurred at low frequency (1.3%) in the whole panel, but was found in many lines in the RGT Planet pedigree (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#MOESM4">3</a>)âincluding commercially successful barley cultivars of past decades, such as Triumph, Quench and Sebastian. The earliest cultivar that carried the inversion was Diamant. As one of the donors of the semi-dwarf growth habit, Diamant was a highly influential founder line of modern barley breeding and traces back to a mutant induced by gamma irradiation of the Czech cultivar Valticky<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Bouma, J. &amp; Ohnoutka, Z. Importance and Application of the Mutant âDiamantâ in Spring Barley Breeding (IAEA, 1991)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1764">36</a></sup>. We genotyped several gene bank accessions and germplasm samples of both Valticky and Diamant. Notably, none of the Valticky samples carried the inversion, whereas it segregated in the Diamant samples (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Fig3">3d</a>). Quantitative trait loci mapping for yield-related traits in the RGT PlanetÂ ÃÂ Hindmarsh population did not show signals on chromosomeÂ 7H (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#MOESM3">2e</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#MOESM14">9</a>), consistent with selective neutrality of the inversion. This strongly suggests that mutation breeding in the 1960s has given rise to a cryptic large inversion, whichâunbeknownst to breedersâsegregates in elite varieties of barley.</p><p>The second inversion we focused on spanned 10Â Mb in the interstitial region of chromosomeÂ 2H (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Fig1">1b</a>) and was present in 26 out of 69Â Hi-C samples (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#MOESM13">8</a>). Local PCA and haplotype analysis in our panel of 200Â domesticated and 100Â wild varieties of barley indicated a single origin of the inverted haplotype (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Fig4">4a, b</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#MOESM3">2c</a>). The inversion occurred only among domesticated barley of Western geographical origin<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Milner, S. G. et al. Genebank genomics highlights the diversity of a global barley collection. Nat. Genet. 51, 319â326 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1793">9</a></sup>, indicating that it arose or has risen to high frequency after domestication. The inverted region contains 46Â high-confidence genes in the Morex cultivar. The closest gene to the inversion breakpointâat 448Â kb distance from the distal breakpoint in the non-carrier Morexâwas <i>HvCENTRORADIALIS</i> (<i>HvCEN</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Comadran, J. et al. Natural variation in a homolog of Antirrhinum CENTRORADIALIS contributed to spring growth habit and environmental adaptation in cultivated barley. Nat. Genet. 44, 1388â1392 (2012)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1804">37</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Fig4">4c</a>). Although induced mutants of <i>HvCEN</i> flower very early, natural variation in <i>HvCEN</i> has previously been implicated in environmental adaptation to northern European climates<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Comadran, J. et al. Natural variation in a homolog of Antirrhinum CENTRORADIALIS contributed to spring growth habit and environmental adaptation in cultivated barley. Nat. Genet. 44, 1388â1392 (2012)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1818">37</a></sup>. All of the inversion carriers we analysed had <i>HvCEN</i> haplotype III, which is associated with later flowering in spring barley varieties from northern Europe<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Comadran, J. et al. Natural variation in a homolog of Antirrhinum CENTRORADIALIS contributed to spring growth habit and environmental adaptation in cultivated barley. Nat. Genet. 44, 1388â1392 (2012)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1825">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Bustos-Korts, D. et al. Exome sequences and multi-environment field trials elucidate the genetic basis of adaptation in barley. Plant J. 99, 1172â1191 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1828">38</a></sup>. Further research is required to determine whether the inversion close to <i>HvCEN</i> has direct functional consequences (for instance, by modulating <i>HvCEN</i> expression) or whether it hitchhiked along with a tightly linked causal variant.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Analysis of a frequent inversion on chromosomeÂ 2H."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Analysis of a frequent inversion on chromosomeÂ 2H.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-020-2947-8/MediaObjects/41586_2020_2947_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-020-2947-8/MediaObjects/41586_2020_2947_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="580" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b>, A PCA showing the localization of inversion carriers in the diversity space of global domesticated barley. The correspondence of PCA coordinates to correlates of population structure is shown in Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Fig5">1</a>. Red dots denote carriers of the inverted haplotype (<i>n</i>Â =Â 87) in a panel of 200Â domesticated varieties of barley. <b>b</b>, PCA for a diversity panel comprising 200Â domesticated (red and green dots) and 100Â wild varieties of barley (blue dots). SNP markers detected in whole-genome shotgun data and located in the inverted regions were used. <b>c</b>, Schematic of the inverted region. The <i>HvCEN</i> gene is closest to the breakpoint that is distal in Morex (distance of 449Â kb) and proximal in Barke (distance of 433Â kb) assemblies. A total of 46 and 44Â high-confidence (HC) genes were annotated in the Morex and Barke assemblies, respectively. The yellow arrows (not drawn to scale) mark the positions of PCR primers to probe for the presence of the inversion (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#MOESM3">2c</a>).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec5" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec5-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec5">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec5-content"><p>The digital representation of the pan-genome can expand the repertoire of natural or induced sequence variation that is accessible to genetic analyses and breeding. Our comparison of 20Â chromosome-scale sequence assemblies has revealed pervasive variation in genes and non-coding regions. Focusing on single-copy sequences, we translated this variation into scorable markers that are amenable to population genetic analysis and association scans. A notable finding was the prevalence of large (more than 5Â Mb in size) inversion polymorphisms in current elite germplasm. It is likely that the suppression of genetic recombination in inversion heterozygotes has manifested itself in hard-to-explain patterns of long-range linkage and segregation distortion between elite lines in breeding programmes. Our map of inversion polymorphisms will provide breeders with a point of reference to avoidâor interpret correctlyâcrosses between carriers and non-carriers. We found abundant structural variation in 20Â representative barley genotypes, but individual events occurred at low frequency (Extended Data Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Fig10">6</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Fig13">9</a>). This observation, combined with the slow saturation of the single-copy pan-genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Fig2">2a</a>), motivate the genomic analysis of more genotypes to expand the barley pan-genome. The next phase of barley pan-genomics will focus on an augmented panel of domesticated and wild germplasm, working towards the long-term goal of high-quality genome sequences of all barley plant genetic resources as part of a biodigital resource centre<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Mascher, M. et al. Genebank genomics bridges the gap between the conservation of crop diversity and plant breeding. Nat. Genet. 51, 1076â1081 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1896">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Khan, A. W. et al. Super-pangenome by integrating the wild side of a species for accelerated crop improvement. Trends Plant Sci. 25, 148â158 (2020)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1899">40</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec6" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec6-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec6">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec6-content"><p>No statistical methods were used to predetermine sample size. The experiments were not randomized, and investigators were not blinded to allocation during experiments and outcome assessment.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec7">Library preparation, sequencing data generation and genome assembly of 20Â diverse varieties of barley</h3><p>High-molecular-weight DNA was extracted from one-week-old seedlings of 20Â diverse barley accessions given in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#MOESM15">10</a>, using a previously described large-scale DNA extraction protocol<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Dvorak, J., McGuire, P. E. &amp; Cassidy, B. Apparent sources of the A genomes of wheats inferred from polymorphism in abundance and restriction fragment length of repeated nucleotide sequences. Genome 30, 680â689 (1988)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1922">41</a></sup>. For the NRGene DeNovoMAGIC3.0 assemblies, 450-bp paired-end (PE450) libraries of Morex, Barke, HOR 10350 and B1K-04-12 were prepared at the Leibniz Institute of Plant Genetics and Crop Plant Research (IPK) Gatersleben. The 450-bp paired-end libraries for other accessions, 800-bp paired-end libraries and mate-pair libraries of three sizes were prepared and sequenced at the University of Illinois Roy J. Carver Biotechnology Center. The 10X Genomics Chromium libraries were prepared at the University of Saskatchewan Wheat Molecular Breeding Laboratoryand sequenced by Genome Quebec or prepared and sequenced at the Roy J. Carver Biotechnology Center, using the manufacturersâ recommendations. Published tethered chromosome conformation data for Morex, Barke, HOR 10350 and B1K-04-12 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Himmelbach, A., Walde, I., Mascher, M. &amp; Stein, N. Tethered chromosome conformation capture sequencing in Triticeae: a valuable tool for genome assembly. Bio Protoc. 8, e2955 (2018)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1926">42</a></sup>) was used for scaffolding the respective genome. For the other accessions, in situ Hi-C libraries were prepared using a previously described method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Padmarasu, S., Himmelbach, A., Mascher, M. &amp; Stein, N. in Plant Long Non-Coding RNAs (eds Chekanova, J. &amp; Wang, H.-L.) 441â472 (Springer, 2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1930">43</a></sup>. Sequencing data generated from each of the libraries are given in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#MOESM15">10</a>. NRGene DeNovoMAGIC3.0 scaffold assemblies were provided for Barke, HOR 10350 and B1K-04-12. The 10X Chromium, population sequencing (POPSEQ) and Hi-C data were then used to prepare chromosome-scale assemblies using the TRITEX pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Monat, C. et al. TRITEX: chromosome-scale sequence assembly of Triticeae genomes with open-source tools. Genome Biol. 20, 284 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1938">6</a></sup> (commit: 7041ff2). For the other assemblies, the TRITEX pipeline was also used for contig assembly and scaffolding with mate-pair and 10X data (Extended Data Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#Tab1">1</a>). High-confidence gene models annotated on the Morex V2 reference<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Monat, C. et al. TRITEX: chromosome-scale sequence assembly of Triticeae genomes with open-source tools. Genome Biol. 20, 284 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1945">6</a></sup> and full-length cDNA sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Matsumoto, T. et al. Comprehensive sequence analysis of 24,783 barley full-length cDNAs derived from 12 clone libraries. Plant Physiol. 156, 20â28 (2011)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1949">44</a></sup> were aligned to the assemblies to assess gene-space completeness with the parameters of â¥90% query coverage and â¥97% (â¥90% for full-length cDNA) identity.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec8">Tissue collection and RNA extraction</h3><p>Plant material for the collection of tissues for RNA sequencing (RNA-seq) and Iso-Seq was grown in the greenhouse at IPK Gatersleben with dayânight temperatures of 21âÂ°Câ18âÂ°C. Embryonic tissue, leaves, roots, internode, inflorescence (5 mm) and developing seeds (5 and 15 days after pollination) were collected as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="International Barley Genome Sequencing Consortium. A physical, genetic and functional sequence assembly of the barley genome. Nature 491, 711â716 (2012)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1961">4</a></sup>, snap-frozen in liquid nitrogen and stored at â80âÂ°C until RNA extractions were performed. RNA was extracted from the collected tissues using a Trizol extraction protocol<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="International Barley Genome Sequencing Consortium. A physical, genetic and functional sequence assembly of the barley genome. Nature 491, 711â716 (2012)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d53489e1965">4</a></sup> and purified using Qiagen RNeasy miniprep columns as per the manufacturerâs instructions. RNA quality was checked on Agilent RNA HS screen tape and RNA with RIN value greater than 8 was used for RNA-seq and Iso-Seq library construction.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec9">RNA-seq library preparation and data generation</h3><p>RNA-seq libraries were prepared from purified RNA using the TruSeq RNA sample preparation kit (Illumina) as per the manufacturerâs recommendation at IPK Gatersleben. Libraries were pooled at equimolar concentrations, quantified by qPCR and paired-end-sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq 2500 for 200 cycles. The data generated for each tissue are given in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#MOESM7">2</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec10">Iso-Seq data generation and analysis</h3><p>Two libraries for each embryonic tissue RNA and pooled RNA from seven tissues (described in âTissue collection and RNA extractionâ) were prepared for Barke and HORÂ 10350 using the PacBio Iso-Seq protocol. In brief, double-stranded RNA was synthesized using SMARTer PCR cDNA synthesis kit (Clontech; cat. no. 634925). Two fractions of cDNA with different size profiles were prepared by using differing ratios of DNA to Ampure XP beads (Beckman Coulter, cat. no. A63882). Equimolar concentration of each fraction were pooled, and a minimum of one microgram of double-stranded cDNA was used for Iso-Seq library construction as per the PacBio library construction protocol. Two additional libraries from pooled RNA tissues were prepared using cDNA prepared from TeloPrime v.1.0 kit (Lexogen) following the manufacturerâs instructions. Libraries were quantified and sequenced on a PacBio Sequel device at IPK Gatersleben. Data were analysed using SMRTLink v.5.0 Isoseq v.1.0 pipeline or Isoseq3 pipeline (<a href="https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/IsoSeq_SA3nUP/wiki/Tutorial:-Installing-and-Running-Iso-Seq-3-using-Conda">https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/IsoSeq_SA3nUP/wiki/Tutorial:-Installing-and-Running-Iso-Seq-3-using-Conda</a>). The steps involved in Iso-Seq data analysis were the generation of circular consensus sequences, and then the classification of circular consensus sequence reads into full-length non-chimeric reads and non-full length reads on the basis of the presence of primer sequences and polyA sequences. Full-length non-chimeric reads were then clustered on the basis of sequence similarity to yield high- and low-quality isoforms. The data generated and method of library preparation are given in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#MOESM7">2</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec11">Gene projections and repeat annotation</h3><p>Gene models for Morex, Barke and HORÂ 10350 were predicted using transcriptome data (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#MOESM7">2</a>) and protein homology evidence, and derived by a previously described annotation pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Mascher, M. et al. A chromosome conformation capture ordered sequence of the barley genome. Nature 544, 427â433 (2017)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d53489e2010">5</a></sup>. High-confidence gene models from these accessions were aligned to pseudo-chromosomes of each accession separately using blat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Kent, W. J. BLATâthe BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656â664 (2002)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d53489e2014">45</a></sup>. For each genomic region identified by blat, additional alignments were performed by exonerate<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Slater, G. S. C. &amp; Birney, E. Automated generation of heuristics for biological sequence comparison. BMC Bioinformatics 6, 31 (2005)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d53489e2018">46</a></sup> in its genomic neighbourhood ranging between 20Â kb upstream and 20Â kb downstream of the match position. A series of quality criteria was applied to select high-confidence gene models in each accession. The functional annotation for genes of 20Â accessions was carried out using the AHRD pipeline v.3.3.3 (<a href="https://github.com/groupschoof/AHRD">https://github.com/groupschoof/AHRD</a>). Orthologous gene groups between the twenty accessions were predicted using OrthoFinder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Emms, D. M. &amp; Kelly, S. OrthoFinder: solving fundamental biases in whole genome comparisons dramatically improves orthogroup inference accuracy. Genome Biol. 16, 157 (2015)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d53489e2030">47</a></sup> v.2.3.1 with default parameters.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec12">Repeat annotation</h3><p>To obtain a consistent transposon annotation across all lines for transposons and tandem repeats, the same methods were applied to all 20 barley lines. Transposons were detected and classified by homology search against the REdat_9.7_Poaceae section of the PGSB transposon library<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Spannagl, M. et al. PGSB PlantsDB: updates to the database framework for comparative plant genome research. Nucleic Acids Res. 44, D1141âD1147 (2016)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2947-8#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d53489e2042">48</a></sup>. The program vmatch (<a href="http://www.vmatch.de">http://www.vmatch.de</a>, version 2.3.0) was used for that purpose as a fast and efficient matching tool that is well-suited for such large and highly repetitive genomes. Vmatch was run with the following parameters: identity â¥ 70%, minimal hit length 75 bp, seed length 12 bp (exact command line: -d -p -l 75 -identity 70 -seedlength 12 -exdrop 5). To remove overlapping annotations, the vmatch output was filtered for redundant hits via a priority-based approach. Higher scoring matches were assigned first and lower scoring hits at overlapping positions were either shortened or removed if they were contained to â¥90% in the overlap or &lt;50 bp of rest length remained. The resulting transposon annotations are overlap-free, but disrupted elements from nested insertions have not been defragmented into one element. Still-intact full-length LTR retrotransposons were identified with LTRharvest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Ellinghaus, D., Kurtz, S. &amp; Willhoeft, U. LTRharvest, an efficient and flexible software for de novo detection of LTR retrotransposons.
